          New settlements by AD 1

      <p>
       <b>
        Gothic
       </b>
       is an
       <a href="/wiki/Extinct_language" title="Extinct language">
        extinct
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/East_Germanic_languages" title="East Germanic languages">
        East
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanic_language" title="Germanic language">
        Germanic language
       </a>
       that was spoken by the
       <a href="/wiki/Goths" title="Goths">
        Goths
       </a>
       . It is known primarily from the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Codex_Argenteus" title="Codex Argenteus">
         Codex Argenteus
        </a>
       </i>
       , a sixth-century copy of a fourth-century Bible translation, and is the only
       <a href="/wiki/East_Germanic_languages" title="East Germanic languages">
        East Germanic language
       </a>
       with a sizable
       <a href="/wiki/Text_corpus" title="Text corpus">
        text corpus
       </a>
       . All others, including
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burgundian_language_(Germanic)" title="Burgundian language (Germanic)">
        Burgundian
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Vandalic_language" title="Vandalic language">
        Vandalic
       </a>
       , are known, if at all, only from proper names that survived in historical accounts, and from
       <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">
        loanwords
       </a>
       in other languages such as
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">
        Portuguese
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       As a Germanic language, Gothic is a part of the
       <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">
        Indo-European language
       </a>
       family. It is the earliest Germanic language that is attested in any sizable texts, but it lacks any modern descendants. The oldest documents in Gothic date back to the fourth century. The language was in decline by the mid-sixth century, partly because of the military defeat of the Goths at the hands of the
       <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">
        Franks
       </a>
       , the elimination of the Goths in Italy, and geographic isolation (in Spain the Gothic language lost its last and probably already declining function as a church language when the
       <a href="/wiki/Visigoths" title="Visigoths">
        Visigoths
       </a>
       converted to Catholicism in 589).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The language survived as a domestic language in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" title="Iberian peninsula">
        Iberian peninsula
       </a>
       (modern Spain and Portugal) as late as the eighth century. Gothic-seeming terms are found in manuscripts subsequent to this date, but these may or may not belong to the same language. In particular, a language known as
       <a href="/wiki/Crimean_Gothic" title="Crimean Gothic">
        Crimean Gothic
       </a>
       survived in the lower
       <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">
        Danube
       </a>
       area and in isolated mountain regions in
       <a href="/wiki/Crimea" title="Crimea">
        Crimea
       </a>
       . Lacking certain sound changes characteristic of Gothic, Crimean Gothic cannot, however, be a lineal descendant of Bible Gothic.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStearns1978118_3-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStearns1978118-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The existence of such early attested texts makes it a language of considerable interest in
       <a href="/wiki/Comparative_linguistics" title="Comparative linguistics">
        comparative linguistics
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History_and_evidence">
        History and evidence
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History and evidence">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ambrosianus.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="946" data-file-width="842" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Ambrosianus.jpg/240px-Ambrosianus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Ambrosianus.jpg/360px-Ambrosianus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Ambrosianus.jpg/480px-Ambrosianus.jpg 2x" width="240"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ambrosianus.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A leaf of the
         <i>
          Codex Ambrosianus B
         </i>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Only a few documents in Gothic survive, not enough for completely reconstructing the language. Most Gothic corpora are translations or glosses of other languages (namely, Greek), so foreign linguistic elements most certainly influenced the texts. These are the primary sources:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The largest body of surviving documentation consists of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Codices" title="Codices">
         codices
        </a>
        written and commissioned by the
        <a href="/wiki/Arianism" title="Arianism">
         Arian
        </a>
        bishop
        <a href="/wiki/Ulfilas" title="Ulfilas">
         Ulfilas
        </a>
        (Wulfila, 311–382), the leader of a community of
        <a href="/wiki/Visigoths" title="Visigoths">
         Visigothic
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">
         Christians
        </a>
        in the
        <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">
         Roman
        </a>
        province of
        <a href="/wiki/Moesia" title="Moesia">
         Moesia
        </a>
        (modern
        <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">
         Bulgaria
        </a>
        /
        <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">
         Romania
        </a>
        ). He commissioned a translation of the
        <a href="/wiki/Greek_Vulgate" title="Greek Vulgate">
         Greek Bible
        </a>
        into the Gothic language, of which roughly three-quarters of the
        <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">
         New Testament
        </a>
        and some fragments of the
        <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">
         Old Testament
        </a>
        have survived. The Ulfilas translations are collected in the following codices:
       </li>
      </ul>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <a href="/wiki/Codex_Argenteus" title="Codex Argenteus">
           Codex Argenteus
          </a>
          (
          <a href="/wiki/Uppsala" title="Uppsala">
           Uppsala
          </a>
          ), including the Speyer fragment: 188 leaves
         </li>
        </ul>
        <dl>
         <dd>
          The best-preserved Gothic manuscript and dating from the sixth century, it was preserved and transmitted by northern
          <a href="/wiki/Ostrogoths" title="Ostrogoths">
           Ostrogoths
          </a>
          in modern Italy. It contains a large part of the four
          <a href="/wiki/Gospel" title="Gospel">
           Gospels
          </a>
          . Since it is a translation from Greek, the language of the
          <i>
           Codex Argenteus
          </i>
          is replete with borrowed Greek words and Greek usages. The syntax in particular is often copied directly from the Greek.
         </dd>
        </dl>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Codex_Ambrosianus" title="Codex Ambrosianus">
            Codex Ambrosianus
           </a>
          </i>
          (
          <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">
           Milan
          </a>
          ) and the
          <i>
           <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Codex_Taurinensis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Codex Taurinensis (page does not exist)">
            Codex Taurinensis
           </a>
          </i>
          (
          <a href="/wiki/Turin" title="Turin">
           Turin
          </a>
          ): Five parts, totaling 193 leaves
         </li>
        </ul>
        <dl>
         <dd>
          It contains scattered passages from the New Testament (including parts of the Gospels and the
          <a href="/wiki/Epistle" title="Epistle">
           Epistles
          </a>
          ), of the
          <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">
           Old Testament
          </a>
          (
          <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Nehemiah" title="Book of Nehemiah">
           Nehemiah
          </a>
          ), and some commentaries known as
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Skeireins" title="Skeireins">
            Skeireins
           </a>
          </i>
          . The text likely had been somewhat modified by copyists.
         </dd>
        </dl>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Codex_Gissensis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Codex Gissensis (page does not exist)">
            Codex Gissensis
           </a>
          </i>
          (
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gie%C3%9Fen" title="Gießen">
           Gießen
          </a>
          ): One leaf, fragments of Luke 23–24 was found in Egypt in 1907 and was destroyed by water damage in 1945.
         </li>
         <li>
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Codex_Carolinus" title="Codex Carolinus">
            Codex Carolinus
           </a>
          </i>
          (
          <a href="/wiki/Wolfenb%C3%BCttel" title="Wolfenbüttel">
           Wolfenbüttel
          </a>
          ): Four leaves, fragments of Romans 11–15
         </li>
         <li>
          <i>
           Codex Vaticanus Latinus
          </i>
          5750: Three leaves, pages 57/58, 59/60, and 61/62 of the
          <i>
           Skeireins
          </i>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <ul>
       <li>
        A scattering of old documents: alphabets, calendars, glosses found in a number of manuscripts and a few
        <a href="/wiki/Gothic_runic_inscriptions" title="Gothic runic inscriptions">
         runic inscriptions
        </a>
        (between three and 13) that are known or suspected to be Gothic: Some scholars believe that these inscriptions are not at all Gothic.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
         <a href="#cite_note-4">
          [4]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Several names in an Indian inscription were thought to be possibly Gothic by Krause.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
         <a href="#cite_note-5">
          [5]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        A small dictionary of more than 80 words and a song without translation, compiled by the Fleming
        <a href="/wiki/Ogier_Ghiselin_de_Busbecq" title="Ogier Ghiselin de Busbecq">
         Ogier Ghiselin de Busbecq
        </a>
        , the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habsburg" title="Habsburg">
         Habsburg
        </a>
        ambassador to the court of the
        <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">
         Ottoman Empire
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">
         Istanbul
        </a>
        from 1555 to 1562, who was curious to find out about the language and by arrangement met two speakers of
        <a href="/wiki/Crimean_Gothic" title="Crimean Gothic">
         Crimean Gothic
        </a>
        and listed the terms in his compilation
        <i>
         Turkish Letters
        </i>
        : These terms date from nearly a millennium later than Ulfilas, so are not representative of his language. Busbecq's material also contains many puzzles and enigmas and is difficult to interpret in the light of comparative Germanic linguistics.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Reports of the discovery of other parts of Ulfilas' Bible have not been substantiated.
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Heinrich_May&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Heinrich May (page does not exist)">
        Heinrich May
       </a>
       in 1968 claimed to have found in England 12 leaves of a
       <a href="/wiki/Palimpsest" title="Palimpsest">
        palimpsest
       </a>
       containing parts of the
       <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Matthew" title="Gospel of Matthew">
        Gospel of Matthew
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Only fragments of the Gothic translation of the
       <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">
        Bible
       </a>
       have been preserved. The translation was apparently done in the
       <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">
        Balkans
       </a>
       region by people in close contact with Greek
       <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">
        Christian
       </a>
       culture. The Gothic Bible apparently was used by the
       <a href="/wiki/Visigoths" title="Visigoths">
        Visigoths
       </a>
       in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" title="Iberian peninsula">
        Iberia
       </a>
       until about 700 AD, and perhaps for a time in Italy, the
       <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">
        Balkans
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">
        Ukraine
       </a>
       . In exterminating
       <a href="/wiki/Arianism" title="Arianism">
        Arianism
       </a>
       , many texts in Gothic were probably expunged and overwritten as palimpsests or collected and burned. Apart from Biblical texts, the only substantial Gothic document that still exists and the only lengthy text known to have been composed originally in the Gothic language, is the
       <i>
        Skeireins
       </i>
       , a few pages of commentary on the
       <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">
        Gospel of John
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Very few secondary sources make reference to the Gothic language after about 800. In
       <i>
        De incrementis ecclesiae Christianae
       </i>
       (840/2),
       <a href="/wiki/Walafrid_Strabo" title="Walafrid Strabo">
        Walafrid Strabo
       </a>
       , a Frankish monk who lived in
       <a href="/wiki/Swabia" title="Swabia">
        Swabia
       </a>
       , speaks of a group of monks, who reported that "even now certain peoples in
       <a href="/wiki/Scythia" title="Scythia">
        Scythia
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Dobruja" title="Dobruja">
        Dobrudja
       </a>
       ), especially around
       <a href="/wiki/Constan%C8%9Ba" title="Constanța">
        Tomis
       </a>
       spoke a
       <i>
        sermo Theotiscus
       </i>
       ('Germanic language'), the language of the Gothic translation of the Bible, and they used such a liturgy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In evaluating medieval texts that mention the
       <a href="/wiki/Goths" title="Goths">
        Goths
       </a>
       , many writers used the word
       <i>
        Goths
       </i>
       to mean any Germanic people in eastern Europe (such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Varangians" title="Varangians">
        Varangians
       </a>
       ), many of whom certainly did not use the Gothic language as known from the Gothic Bible. Some writers even referred to
       <a href="/wiki/Slavic_languages" title="Slavic languages">
        Slavic
       </a>
       -speaking people as Goths. However, it is clear from Ulfilas' translation that despite some puzzles the language belongs with the Germanic language group, not with Slavonic.
      </p>
      <p>
       The relationship between the language of the Crimean Goths and Ulfilas's Gothic is less clear. The few fragments of Crimean Gothic from the 16th century show significant differences from the language of the Gothic Bible although some of the glosses, such as
       <i>
        ada
       </i>
       for "egg", could indicate a common heritage, and Gothic
       <i>
        mena
       </i>
       ("moon"), compared to Crimean Gothic
       <i>
        mine
       </i>
       , can suggest an East Germanic connection.
      </p>
      <p>
       Generally, the Gothic language refers to the language of
       <a href="/wiki/Ulfilas" title="Ulfilas">
        Ulfilas
       </a>
       , but the attestations themselves are largely from the 6th century, long after Ulfilas had died. The above list is not exhaustive, and a more extensive list is available on the website of the Wulfila project.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Alphabet_and_transliteration">
        Alphabet and transliteration
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Alphabet and transliteration">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Gothic_alphabet" title="Gothic alphabet">
        Gothic alphabet
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Ulfilas's Gothic, as well as that of the
       <i>
        Skeireins
       </i>
       and various other manuscripts, was written using an alphabet that was most likely invented by Ulfilas himself for his translation. Some scholars (such as Braune) claim that it was derived from the
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">
        Greek alphabet
       </a>
       only while others maintain that there are some Gothic letters of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Runic" title="Runic">
        Runic
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       origin.
      </p>
      <p>
       This
       <a href="/wiki/Gothic_alphabet" title="Gothic alphabet">
        Gothic alphabet
       </a>
       has nothing to do either with
       <a href="/wiki/Blackletter" title="Blackletter">
        blackletter
       </a>
       (also called
       <i>
        Gothic script
       </i>
       ), which was used to write the
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">
        Latin script
       </a>
       from the 12th to 14th centuries and evolved into the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fraktur_(typeface_sub-classification)" title="Fraktur (typeface sub-classification)">
        Fraktur
       </a>
       writing later used to write
       <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">
        German
       </a>
       or with the
       <a href="/wiki/Sans-serif" title="Sans-serif">
        sans-serif
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_font" title="Type font">
        type fonts
       </a>
       sometimes called "Gothic".
      </p>
      <p>
       A standardized system is used for transliterating Gothic words into the
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">
        Latin script
       </a>
       . The system mirrors the conventions of the native alphabet, such as writing long
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /iː/
       </span>
       as
       <i>
        ei
       </i>
       . The Goths used their equivalents of
       <i>
        e
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        o
       </i>
       alone only for long higher vowels, using the digraphs
       <i>
        ai
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        au
       </i>
       (much as in
       <a href="/wiki/French_orthography" title="French orthography">
        French
       </a>
       ) for the corresponding short or lower vowels. There are two variant spelling systems: a "raw" one that directly transliterates the original Gothic script and a "normalized" one that adds
       <a href="/wiki/Diacritic" title="Diacritic">
        diacritics
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Macron_(diacritic)" title="Macron (diacritic)">
        macrons
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Acute_accent" title="Acute accent">
        acute accents
       </a>
       ) to certain vowels to clarify the pronunciation or, in certain cases, to indicate the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" title="Proto-Germanic">
        Proto-Germanic
       </a>
       origin of the vowel in question. The latter system is usually used in the academic literature.
      </p>
      <p>
       The following table shows the correspondence between spelling and sound for vowels:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Gothic letter
         <br/>
         or
         <a href="/wiki/Digraph_(orthography)" title="Digraph (orthography)">
          digraph
         </a>
        </th>
        <th>
         Roman
         <br/>
         equivalent
        </th>
        <th>
         "Normalised"
         <br/>
         transliteration
        </th>
        <th>
         Sound
        </th>
        <th>
         Normal environment of occurrence

      <p>
       Notes:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        This "normalised transliteration" system devised by
        <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Grimm" title="Jacob Grimm">
         Jacob Grimm
        </a>
        is used in some modern editions of Gothic texts and in studies of
        <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic_language" title="Proto-Germanic language">
         Common Germanic
        </a>
        . It signals distinctions not made by Ulfilas in his alphabet. Rather, they reflect various origins in Proto-Germanic. Thus,
        <ul>
         <li>
          <big>
           <i>
            aí
           </i>
          </big>
          is used for the sound derived from the Proto-Germanic short vowels
          <i>
           e
          </i>
          and
          <i>
           i
          </i>
          before
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           /h/
          </span>
          and
          <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
           /r/
          </span>
          .
         </li>
         <li>
          <big>
           <i>
            ái
           </i>
          </big>
          is used for the sound derived from the Proto-Germanic diphthong
          <i>
           ai
          </i>
          . Some scholars have considered this sound to have remained as a diphthong in Gothic. However, Ulfilas was highly consistent in other spelling inventions, which makes it unlikely that he assigned two different sounds to the same digraph. Furthermore, he consistently used the digraph to represent Greek
          <i>
           αι
          </i>
          , which was then certainly a
          <a href="/wiki/Monophthong" title="Monophthong">
           monophthong
          </a>
          . A monophthongal value is accepted by
          <a href="/wiki/Eduard_Prokosch" title="Eduard Prokosch">
           Eduard Prokosch
          </a>
          in his influential
          <i>
           A Common Germanic Grammar
          </i>
          .
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prokosch_7-0">
           <a href="#cite_note-Prokosch-7">
            [7]
           </a>
          </sup>
          It had earlier been accepted by
          <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Wright_(linguist)" title="Joseph Wright (linguist)">
           Joseph Wright
          </a>
          but only in an appendix to his
          <i>
           Grammar of the Gothic Language
          </i>
          .
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wright_8-0">
           <a href="#cite_note-Wright-8">
            [8]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </li>
         <li>
          <big>
           <i>
            ai
           </i>
          </big>
          is used for the sound derived from the Common Germanic long vowel
          <i>
           ē
          </i>
          before a vowel.
         </li>
         <li>
          <big>
           <i>
            áu
           </i>
          </big>
          is used for the sound derived from Common Germanic diphthong
          <i>
           au
          </i>
          . It cannot be related to a Greek digraph, since
          <i>
           αυ
          </i>
          then represented a sequence of vowel+spiral (
          <a href="/wiki/Fricative_consonant" title="Fricative consonant">
           fricative
          </a>
          ) consonant, which Ulfilas transcribed as (
          <i>
           aw
          </i>
          in "normalised transcription") in representing Greek words. Nevertheless, the argument based on simplicity is accepted by some influential scholars.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prokosch_7-1">
           <a href="#cite_note-Prokosch-7">
            [7]
           </a>
          </sup>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wright_8-1">
           <a href="#cite_note-Wright-8">
            [8]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        The "normal environment of occurrence" refers to native words. In foreign words, these environments are often greatly disturbed. For example, the short sounds
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɛ/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /i/
        </span>
        alternate in native words in a nearly allophonic way, with
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɛ/
        </span>
        occurring in native words only before the consonants
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /h/
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /hʷ/
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /r/
        </span>
        while
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /i/
        </span>
        occurs everywhere else (nevertheless, there are a few exceptions such as
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /i/
        </span>
        before
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /r/
        </span>
        in
        <i>
         hiri
        </i>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɛ/
        </span>
        consistently in the
        <a href="/wiki/Reduplication" title="Reduplication">
         reduplicating
        </a>
        syllable of certain past-tense verbs regardless of the following consonant, which indicate that these sounds had become phonemicized). In foreign borrowings, however,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɛ/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /i/
        </span>
        occur freely in all environments, reflecting the corresponding vowel quality in the source language.
       </li>
       <li>
        Paradigmatic alterations can occur either intra-paradigm (between two different forms within a specific paradigm) or cross-paradigm (between the same form in two different paradigms of the same class). Examples of intra-paradigm alternation are
        <i>
         gawi
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɡa.wi/
        </span>
        "district (nom.)" vs.
        <i>
         gáujis
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɡɔː.jis/
        </span>
        "district (gen.)";
        <i>
         mawi
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ma.wi/
        </span>
        "maiden (nom.)" vs.
        <i>
         máujōs
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /mɔː.joːs/
        </span>
        "maiden (gen.)";
        <i>
         þiwi
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /θi.wi/
        </span>
        "maiden (nom.)" vs.
        <i>
         þiujōs
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /θiu.joːs/
        </span>
        "maiden (gen.)";
        <i>
         taui
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /tɔː.i/
        </span>
        "deed (nom.)" vs.
        <i>
         tōjis
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /toː.jis/
        </span>
        "deed (gen.)";
        <i>
         náus
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /nɔːs/
        </span>
        "corpse (nom.)" vs.
        <i>
         naweis
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /na.wiːs/
        </span>
        "corpses (nom.)";
        <i>
         triu
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /triu/
        </span>
        ?? "tree (nom.)" vs.
        <i>
         triwis
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /tri.wis/
        </span>
        "tree (gen.)";
        <i>
         táujan
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /tɔː.jan/
        </span>
        "to do" vs.
        <i>
         tawida
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ta.wi.ða/
        </span>
        "I/he did";
        <i>
         stōjan
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /stoː.jan/
        </span>
        "to judge" vs.
        <i>
         stauida
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /stɔː.i.ða/
        </span>
        "I/he judged". Examples of cross-paradigm alternation are Class IV verbs
        <i>
         qiman
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /kʷiman/
        </span>
        "to come" vs.
        <i>
         baíran
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /bɛran/
        </span>
        "to carry",
        <i>
         qumans
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /kʷumans/
        </span>
        "(having) come" vs.
        <i>
         baúrans
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /bɔrans/
        </span>
        "(having) carried"; Class VIIb verbs
        <i>
         lētan
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /leː.tan/
        </span>
        "to let" vs.
        <i>
         saian
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /sɛː.an/
        </span>
        "to sow" (note similar preterites
        <i>
         laílōt
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /lɛ.loːt/
        </span>
        "I/he let",
        <i>
         saísō
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /sɛ.soː/
        </span>
        "I/he sowed"). A combination of intra- and cross-paradigm alternation occurs in Class V
        <i>
         sniwan
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /sni.wan/
        </span>
        "to hasten" vs.
        <i>
         snáu
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /snɔː/
        </span>
        "I/he hastened" (expected *
        <i>
         snaw
        </i>
        , cf.
        <i>
         qiman
        </i>
        "to come",
        <i>
         qam
        </i>
        "I/he came").
       </li>
       <li>
        The carefully maintained alternations between
        <i>
         iu
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         iw
        </i>
        suggest that
        <i>
         iu
        </i>
        may have been something other than
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /iu/
        </span>
        . Various possibilities have been suggested (for example, high central or high back unrounded vowels, such as
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɨ] [ʉ] [ɯ]
        </span>
        ); under these theories, the spelling of
        <i>
         iu
        </i>
        is derived from the fact that the sound alternates with
        <i>
         iw
        </i>
        before a vowel, based on the similar alternations
        <i>
         au
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         aw
        </i>
        . The most common theory, however, simply posits
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /iu/
        </span>
        as the pronunciation of
        <i>
         iu
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Macrons represent long
        <i>
         ā
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         ū
        </i>
        (however, long i appears as
        <i>
         ei
        </i>
        , following the representation used in the native alphabet). Macrons are often also used in the case of
        <i>
         ē
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         ō
        </i>
        ; however, they are sometimes omitted since these vowels are always long. Long
        <i>
         ā
        </i>
        occurs only before the consonants
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /h/
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /hʷ/
        </span>
        and represents
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" title="Proto-Germanic">
         Proto-Germanic
        </a>
        nasalized
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ãː(h)/
        </span>
        &lt; earlier
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /aŋ(h)/
        </span>
        , still non-nasal
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /aː/
        </span>
        did not occur in Proto-Germanic. It is possible that the Gothic vowel still preserved the nasalization, or else that the nasalization was lost but the length distinction kept, as has happened with
        <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">
         Lithuanian
        </a>
        ą. Non-nasal
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /iː/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /uː/
        </span>
        occurred in Proto-Germanic, however and so long
        <i>
         ei
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         ū
        </i>
        occur in all contexts. Before
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /h/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /hʷ/
        </span>
        , long
        <i>
         ei
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         ū
        </i>
        could stem from either non-nasal or nasal long vowels in Proto-Germanic; it is possible that the nasalization was still preserved in Gothc but not written.
       </li>
      </ul>

        </td>

         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /
          <a href="/wiki/Labialization" title="Labialization">
           kʷ
          </a>
          /
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [
          <a href="/wiki/Labialization" title="Labialization">
           kʷ
          </a>
          ]
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         Everywhere except before a voiced consonant
        </td>
        <td>
         –
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /kʷ/
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         𐍂
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          r
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_trill" title="Alveolar trill">
           r
          </a>
          /
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_trill" title="Alveolar trill">
           r
          </a>
          ]
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         Everywhere
        </td>
        <td>
         –
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /r/
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         𐍃
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          s
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /
          <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_fricative#Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiceless alveolar fricative">

          </a>

         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [

          </a>
          ]
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         Everywhere except before a voiced consonant
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">

        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">

         </span>
         ;
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /z/
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         𐍄
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          t
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /
          <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_dental_and_alveolar_stops#Alveolar" title="Voiceless dental and alveolar stops">
           t
          </a>
          /
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">

          <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_dental_and_alveolar_stops#Alveolar" title="Voiceless dental and alveolar stops">
           t
          </a>
          ]
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         Everywhere except before a voiced consonant
        </td>
        <td>

        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /t/
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         𐌸
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          þ
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /

          </a>

        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">

          </a>
          ]
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         Everywhere except before a voiced consonant
        </td>

          /d/
         </span>

          [ð]
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">

         </span>
         ;
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /d/
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         𐍅
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          w
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /
          <a href="/wiki/Voiced_labio-velar_approximant" title="Voiced labio-velar approximant">
           w
          </a>
          /
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">

          </a>
          ]
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         Everywhere
        </td>
        <td>
         –
        </td>

         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /w/
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         𐌶
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          z
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /
          <a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_fricative#Voiced_alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiced alveolar fricative">
           z
          </a>
          /
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          [
          <a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_fricative#Voiced_alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiced alveolar fricative">
           z
          </a>
          ]
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         After a vowel, before a voiced sound
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /s/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /z/
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /hʷ/
        </span>
        , which is written with a single character in the native alphabet, is transliterated using the symbol
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Hwair" title="Hwair">
          ƕ
         </a>
        </i>
        , which is used only in transliterating Gothic.
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /kʷ/
        </span>
        is similarly written with a single character in the native alphabet and is transliterated
        <i>
         q
        </i>
        (with no following
        <i>
         u
        </i>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɡʷ/
        </span>
        , however, is written with two letters in the native alphabet and hence
        <i>
         gw
        </i>
        . The lack of a single letter to represent this sound may result from its restricted distribution (only after
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /n/
        </span>
        ) and its rarity.
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /θ/
        </span>
        is written
        <i>
         þ
        </i>
        , similarly to other Germanic languages.
       </li>
       <li>
        Although
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ŋ]
        </span>
        is the
        <a href="/wiki/Allophone" title="Allophone">
         allophone
        </a>
        of
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /n/
        </span>
        occurring before
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɡ/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /k/
        </span>
        , it is written
        <i>
         g
        </i>
        , following the native alphabet convention (which, in turn, follows Greek usage), which leads to occasional ambiguities, e.g.
        <i>
         saggws
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [saŋɡʷs]
        </span>
        "song" but
        <i>
         triggws
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [triɡɡʷs]
        </span>
        "faithful" (cf. English "true").
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">
        Phonology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Phonology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       It is possible to determine more or less exactly how the Gothic of
       <a href="/wiki/Ulfilas" title="Ulfilas">
        Ulfilas
       </a>
       was pronounced, primarily through comparative phonetic reconstruction. Furthermore, because Ulfilas tried to follow the original Greek text as much as possible in his translation, it is known that he used the same writing conventions as those of contemporary Greek. Since the Greek of that period is well documented, it is possible to reconstruct much of Gothic pronunciation from translated texts. In addition, the way in which non-Greek names are transcribed in the Greek Bible and in Ulfilas's Bible is very informative.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">
        Vowels
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Gothic_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Vowels">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <table>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center">
          <caption>
           Short vowels
          </caption>
          <tr>
           <th>

            </span>

           </th>
           <td>
            <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
             ɛː
            </span>
           </td>
           <td>
            <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
             ɔː
            </span>
           </td>
          </tr>
          <tr>
           <th>
            <a href="/wiki/Open_vowel" title="Open vowel">
             Open
            </a>
           </th>
           <td colspan="2">
            <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
             aː
            </span>
           </td>
          </tr>
         </table>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /a/
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /i/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /u/
        </span>
        can be either long or short.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
         <a href="#cite_note-9">
          [9]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Gothic writing distinguishes between long and short vowels only for
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /i/
        </span>
        by writing
        <i>
         i
        </i>
        for the short form and
        <i>
         ei
        </i>
        for the long (a
        <a href="/wiki/Digraph_(orthography)" title="Digraph (orthography)">
         digraph
        </a>
        or
        <i>
         false diphthong
        </i>
        ), in an imitation of Greek usage (ει =
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /iː/
        </span>
        ). Single vowels are sometimes long where a historically present
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nasal_stop" title="Nasal stop">
         nasal consonant
        </a>
        has been dropped in front of an
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /h/
        </span>
        (a case of
        <a href="/wiki/Compensatory_lengthening" title="Compensatory lengthening">
         compensatory lengthening
        </a>
        ). Thus, the preterite of the verb
        <i>
         briggan
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [briŋɡan]
        </span>
        "to bring" (English
        <i>
         bring
        </i>
        , Dutch
        <i>
         brengen
        </i>
        , German
        <i>
         bringen
        </i>
        ) becomes
        <i>
         brahta
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [braːxta]
        </span>
        (English
        <i>
         brought
        </i>
        , Dutch
        <i>
         bracht
        </i>
        , German
        <i>
         brachte
        </i>
        ), from
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" title="Proto-Germanic">
         Proto-Germanic
        </a>
        *
        <i>
         branhtē
        </i>
        . In detailed
        <a href="/wiki/Transliteration" title="Transliteration">
         transliteration
        </a>
        , when the intent is more
        <a href="/wiki/Phonetic_transcription" title="Phonetic transcription">
         phonetic transcription
        </a>
        , length is noted by a
        <a href="/wiki/Macron_(diacritic)" title="Macron (diacritic)">
         macron
        </a>
        (or failing that, often a
        <a href="/wiki/Circumflex" title="Circumflex">
         circumflex
        </a>
        ):
        <i>
         brāhta
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         brâhta
        </i>
        . This is the only context in which
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /aː/
        </span>
        appears natively whereas
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /uː/
        </span>
        , like
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /iː/
        </span>
        , is found often enough in other contexts:
        <i>
         brūks
        </i>
        "useful" (Dutch
        <i>
         gebruik
        </i>
        , German
        <i>
         Gebrauch
        </i>
        , Icelandic
        <i>
         brúk
        </i>
        "use").
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /eː/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /oː/
        </span>
        are long
        <a href="/wiki/Close-mid_vowel" title="Close-mid vowel">
         close-mid vowels
        </a>
        . They are written as
        <i>
         e
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         o
        </i>
        :
        <i>
         neƕ
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [neːʍ]
        </span>
        "near" (English
        <i>
         nigh
        </i>
        , Dutch
        <i>
         nader
        </i>
        , German
        <i>
         nah
        </i>
        );
        <i>
         fodjan
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [foːdjan]
        </span>
        "to feed".
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɛ/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɔ/
        </span>
        are short
        <a href="/wiki/Open-mid_vowel" title="Open-mid vowel">
         open-mid vowels
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
         <a href="#cite_note-10">
          [10]
         </a>
        </sup>
        They are noted using the digraphs
        <i>
         ai
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         au
        </i>
        :
        <i>
         taihun
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [tɛhun]
        </span>
        "ten" (Dutch
        <i>
         tien
        </i>
        , German
        <i>
         zehn
        </i>
        , Icelandic
        <i>
         tíu
        </i>
        ),
        <i>
         dauhtar
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [dɔxtar]
        </span>
        "daughter" (Dutch
        <i>
         dochter
        </i>
        , German
        <i>
         Tochter
        </i>
        , Icelandic
        <i>
         dóttir
        </i>
        ). In transliterating Gothic, accents are placed on the second vowel of these digraphs
        <i>
         aí
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         aú
        </i>
        to distinguish them from the original diphthongs
        <i>
         ái
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         áu
        </i>
        :
        <i>
         taíhun
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         daúhtar
        </i>
        . In most cases short
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɛ]
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɔ]
        </span>
        are allophones of
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /i, u/
        </span>
        before
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /r, h, ʍ/
        </span>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
         <a href="#cite_note-11">
          [11]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Furthermore, the reduplication syllable of the reduplicating preterites has
        <i>
         ai
        </i>
        as well, which was probably pronounced as a short
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɛ]
        </span>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
         <a href="#cite_note-12">
          [12]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Finally, short
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɛ]
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɔ]
        </span>
        occur in loan words from Greek and Latin (
        <i>
         aípiskaúpus
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɛpiskɔpus]
        </span>
        =
        <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
         ἐπίσκοπος
        </span>
        "bishop",
        <i>
         laíktjo
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [lɛktjoː]
        </span>
        =
        <i>
         lectio
        </i>
        "lection",
        <i>
         Paúntius
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [pɔntius]
        </span>
        =
        <i>
         Pontius
        </i>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        The Germanic
        <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">
         diphthongs
        </a>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ai/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /au/
        </span>
        appear as
        <a href="/wiki/Digraph_(orthography)" title="Digraph (orthography)">
         digraphs
        </a>
        written ⟨ai⟩ and ⟨au⟩ in Gothic. Researchers have disagreed over whether they were still pronounced as diphthongs
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ai̯/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /au̯/
        </span>
        in Ulfilas's time (4th century AD) or had become long
        <a href="/wiki/Open-mid_vowel" title="Open-mid vowel">
         open-mid vowels
        </a>
        :
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɛː/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɔː/
        </span>
        :
        <i>
         ains
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ains] / [ɛːns]
        </span>
        "one" (German
        <i>
         eins
        </i>
        , Icelandic
        <i>
         einn
        </i>
        ),
        <i>
         augo
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [auɣoː] / [ɔːɣoː]
        </span>
        "eye" (German
        <i>
         Auge
        </i>
        , Icelandic
        <i>
         auga
        </i>
        ). It is most likely that the latter view is correct, as it is indisputable that the digraphs ⟨ai⟩ and ⟨au⟩ represent the sounds
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɛː/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɔː/
        </span>
        in some circumstances (see below), and ⟨aj⟩ and ⟨aw⟩ were available to unambiguously represent the sounds
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ai̯/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /au̯/
        </span>
        . The digraph ⟨aw⟩ is in fact used to represent
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /au/
        </span>
        in foreign words (such as
        <i>
         Pawlus
        </i>
        "Paul"), and alternations between ⟨ai⟩/⟨aj⟩ and ⟨au⟩/⟨aw⟩ are scrupulously maintained in paradigms were both variants occur (e.g.
        <i>
         taujan
        </i>
        "to do" vs. past tense
        <i>
         tawida
        </i>
        "did"). Evidence from transcriptions of Gothic names into Latin suggests that the sound change had occurred very recently when Gothic spelling was standardized: Gothic names with Germanic
        <i>
         au
        </i>
        are rendered with
        <i>
         au
        </i>
        in Latin until the 4th century and
        <i>
         o
        </i>
        later on (
        <i>
         Austrogoti
        </i>
        &gt;
        <i>
         Ostrogoti
        </i>
        ). The digraphs ⟨ai⟩ and ⟨au⟩ are normally written with an accent on the first vowel (
        <i>
         ái, áu
        </i>
        ) when they correspond to Proto-Germanic
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ai̯/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /au̯/
        </span>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Long
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɛː]
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɔː]
        </span>
        also occur as allophones of
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /eː/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /uː, oː/
        </span>
        respectively before a following vowel:
        <i>
         waian
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [wɛːan]
        </span>
        "to blow" (Dutch
        <i>
         waaien
        </i>
        , German
        <i>
         wehen
        </i>
        ),
        <i>
         bauan
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [bɔːan]
        </span>
        "to build" (Dutch
        <i>
         bouwen
        </i>
        , German "bauen", Icelandic
        <i>
         búa
        </i>
        "to live, reside"), also in Greek words
        <i>
         Trauada
        </i>
        "Troad" (Gk.
        <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
         Τρῳάς
        </span>
        ). In detailed transcription these are notated
        <i>
         ai, au
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /y/
        </span>
        (pronounced like German
        <i>
         ü
        </i>
        and French
        <i>
         u
        </i>
        ) is a Greek sound used only in borrowed words. It is transliterated as
        <i>
         w
        </i>
        (as it uses the same letter that otherwise denoted the consonant
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /w/
        </span>
        ):
        <i>
         azwmus
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [azymus]
        </span>
        "unleavened bread" ( &lt; Gk.
        <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
         ἄζυμος
        </span>
        ). It represents an υ (y) or the diphthong οι (oi), both of which were pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [y]
        </span>
        in the Greek of the time. Since the sound was foreign to Gothic, it was perhaps pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [i]
        </span>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /iu/
        </span>
        is a falling
        <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">
         diphthong
        </a>
        (
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [iu̯]
        </span>
        :
        <i>
         diups
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [diu̯ps]
        </span>
        "deep" (Dutch
        <i>
         diep
        </i>
        , German
        <i>
         tief
        </i>
        , Icelandic
        <i>
         djúpur
        </i>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        Greek diphthongs: In
        <a href="/wiki/Ulfilas" title="Ulfilas">
         Ulfilas
        </a>
        's era, all the diphthongs of Classical Greek had become simple vowels in speech (
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Monophthong" title="Monophthong">
          monophthongization
         </a>
        </i>
        ), except for αυ (au) and ευ (eu), which were probably still pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [aβ]
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɛβ]
        </span>
        . (They evolved into
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [av~af]
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ev~ef]
        </span>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">
         Modern Greek
        </a>
        .) Ulfilas notes them, in words borrowed from Greek, as
        <i>
         aw
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         aiw
        </i>
        , probably pronounced
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [au̯, ɛu̯]
        </span>
        :
        <i>
         Pawlus
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [pau̯lus]
        </span>
        "Paul" (Gk.
        <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
         Παῦλος
        </span>
        ),
        <i>
         aíwaggelista
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [ɛwaŋɡeːlista]
        </span>
        "evangelist" (Gk.
        <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
         εὐαγγελιστής
        </span>
        , via the Latin
        <i>
         evangelista
        </i>
        ).
       </li>
       <li>
        All vowels (including diphthongs) can be followed by a
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [w]
        </span>
        , which was likely pronounced as the second element of a diphthong with roughly the sound of
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [u̯]
        </span>
        . It seems likely that this is more of an instance of phonetic juxtaposition than of true diphthongs (such as, for example, the sound
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /aj/
        </span>
        in the French word
        <i>
         paille
        </i>
        ("straw"), which is not the diphthong
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ai̯/
        </span>
        but rather a vowel followed by an
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Approximant" title="Approximant">
         approximant
        </a>
        ):
        <i>
         alew
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [aleːw]
        </span>
        "olive oil" ( &lt; Latin
        <i>
         oleum
        </i>
        ),
        <i>
         snáiws
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [snɛːws]
        </span>
        ("snow"),
        <i>
         lasiws
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [lasiws]
        </span>
        "tired" (English
        <i>
         lazy
        </i>
        ).
       </li>
      </ul>

         </i>

         </span>

         </i>

         </span>

         </i>

         </span>

         </i>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
